1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/%D0%9A%D1%80%D1%83%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BF%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B0_%D0%9C%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B2%D1%8B_%D1%81%D0%BE_%D0%A1%D0%BF%D0%B0%D1%81%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D0%B1%D0%B0%D1%88%D0%BD%D0%B8_%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BB%D1%8F.jpg/3922px-%D0%9A%D1%80%D1%83%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BF%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B0_%D0%9C%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B2%D1%8B_%D1%81%D0%BE_%D0%A1%D0%BF%D0%B0%D1%81%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D0%B1%D0%B0%D1%88%D0%BD%D0%B8_%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BB%D1%8F.jpg 2x" width="1961"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%9A%D1%80%D1%83%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%BF%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B0_%D0%9C%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B2%D1%8B_%D1%81%D0%BE_%D0%A1%D0%BF%D0%B0%D1%81%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D0%B1%D0%B0%D1%88%D0%BD%D0%B8_%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BB%D1%8F.jpg" title="File:Круговая панорама Москвы со Спасской башни Кремля.jpg"> </a></div><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">A <a href="/wiki/Panorama" title="Panorama">panoramic</a> view of Moscow from the <a href="/wiki/Spasskaya_Tower" title="Spasskaya Tower">Spasskaya Tower</a> in 1819–1823</div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moscou._Le_Kremlin_LOC_ppmsca.52725.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3774" data-file-width="5167" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Moscou._Le_Kremlin_LOC_ppmsca.52725.jpg/230px-Moscou._Le_Kremlin_LOC_ppmsca.52725.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Moscou._Le_Kremlin_LOC_ppmsca.52725.jpg/345px-Moscou._Le_Kremlin_LOC_ppmsca.52725.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Moscou._Le_Kremlin_LOC_ppmsca.52725.jpg/460px-Moscou._Le_Kremlin_LOC_ppmsca.52725.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moscou._Le_Kremlin_LOC_ppmsca.52725.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Moskva_(river)" title="Moskva (river)">Moskva</a> riverfront in the 19th century</div></div></div>
<p>After losing the status as the capital of the empire, the population of Moscow at first decreased, from 200,000 in the 17th century to 130,000 in 1750. But after 1750, the population grew more than tenfold over the remaining duration of the Russian Empire, reaching 1.8 million by 1915. The <a href="/wiki/1770%E2%80%931772_Russian_plague" title="1770–1772 Russian plague">1770–1772 Russian plague</a> killed up to 100,000 people in Moscow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kniznije_lavki.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1300" data-file-width="1738" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Kniznije_lavki.jpg/220px-Kniznije_lavki.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Kniznije_lavki.jpg/330px-Kniznije_lavki.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Kniznije_lavki.jpg/440px-Kniznije_lavki.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kniznije_lavki.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bookshops at the <a href="/wiki/Novospassky_Bridge" title="Novospassky Bridge">Novospassky Bridge</a> in the 17th century, by <a href="/wiki/Apollinary_Vasnetsov" title="Apollinary Vasnetsov">Apollinary Vasnetsov</a></div></div></div>
<p>By 1700, the building of cobbled roads had begun. In November 1730, the permanent street light was introduced, and by 1867 many streets had a gaslight. In 1883, near the Prechistinskiye Gates, arc lamps were installed. In 1741 Moscow was surrounded by a barricade 40 kilometres (25 mi) long, the Kamer-Kollezhskiy barrier, with 16 gates at which customs tolls were collected. Its line is traced today by a number of streets called <i>val</i> (“ramparts”).
Between 1781 and 1804 the Mytischinskiy water pipe (the first in Russia) was built. In 1813, following the destruction of much of the city during the French occupation, a Commission for the Construction of the City of Moscow was established. It launched a great program of rebuilding, including a partial replanning of the city-centre. Among many buildings constructed or reconstructed at this time was the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Kremlin_Palace" title="Grand Kremlin Palace">Grand Kremlin Palace</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kremlin_Armoury" title="Kremlin Armoury">Kremlin Armoury</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moscow_University" title="Moscow University">Moscow University</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Manege" title="Moscow Manege">Moscow Manege</a> (Riding School), and the <a href="/wiki/Bolshoi_Theatre" title="Bolshoi Theatre">Bolshoi Theatre</a>. In 1903 the Moskvoretskaya water supply was completed.
</p><p>In the early 19th century, the Arch of Konstantino-Elenensky gate was paved with bricks, but the Spassky Gate was the main front gate of the Kremlin and used for royal entrances. From this gate, wooden and (following the 17th-century improvements) stone bridges stretched across the moat. Books were sold on this bridge and stone platforms were built nearby for guns – "raskats". The <a href="/wiki/Tsar_Cannon" title="Tsar Cannon">Tsar Cannon</a> was located on the platform of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lobnoye_mesto" title="Lobnoye mesto">Lobnoye mesto</a>.
</p><p>The road connecting Moscow with St. Petersburg, now the <a href="/wiki/M10_highway_(Russia)" title="M10 highway (Russia)">M10 highway</a>, was completed in 1746, its Moscow end following the old <a href="/wiki/Tver" title="Tver">Tver</a> road, which had existed since the 16th century. It became known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leningradsky_Prospekt" title="Leningradsky Prospekt">Peterburskoye Schosse</a></i> after it was paved in the 1780s. <a href="/wiki/Petrovsky_Palace" title="Petrovsky Palace">Petrovsky Palace</a> was built in 1776–1780 by <a href="/wiki/Matvey_Kazakov" title="Matvey Kazakov">Matvey Kazakov</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fireofmoscow.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="496" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Fireofmoscow.jpg/220px-Fireofmoscow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Fireofmoscow.jpg/330px-Fireofmoscow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Fireofmoscow.jpg/440px-Fireofmoscow.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fireofmoscow.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Napoleon retreating from the city during the <a href="/wiki/Fire_of_Moscow_(1812)" title="Fire of Moscow (1812)">Fire of Moscow</a>, after the failed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Invasion_of_Russia" title="French Invasion of Russia">French Invasion of Russia</a></div></div></div>
<p>When <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" title="Napoleon I of France">Napoleon</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon%27s_invasion_of_Russia" title="Napoleon's invasion of Russia">invaded</a> Russia in 1812, the Moscovites were evacuated. It is suspected that the <a href="/wiki/Fire_of_Moscow_(1812)" title="Fire of Moscow (1812)">Moscow fire</a> was principally the effect of Russian sabotage. Napoleon's <i><a href="/wiki/Grande_Arm%C3%A9e" title="Grande Armée">Grande Armée</a></i> was forced to retreat and was nearly annihilated by the devastating Russian winter and sporadic attacks by Russian military forces. As many as 400,000 of Napoleon's soldiers died during this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alekseev_Illuminciy_na_Sobornoy_pl_v_chest_koronacii_Alexandra_I.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1418" data-file-width="1919" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Alekseev_Illuminciy_na_Sobornoy_pl_v_chest_koronacii_Alexandra_I.jpg/220px-Alekseev_Illuminciy_na_Sobornoy_pl_v_chest_koronacii_Alexandra_I.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Alekseev_Illuminciy_na_Sobornoy_pl_v_chest_koronacii_Alexandra_I.jpg/330px-Alekseev_Illuminciy_na_Sobornoy_pl_v_chest_koronacii_Alexandra_I.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Alekseev_Illuminciy_na_Sobornoy_pl_v_chest_koronacii_Alexandra_I.jpg/440px-Alekseev_Illuminciy_na_Sobornoy_pl_v_chest_koronacii_Alexandra_I.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alekseev_Illuminciy_na_Sobornoy_pl_v_chest_koronacii_Alexandra_I.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cathedral_Square,_Moscow" title="Cathedral Square, Moscow">Cathedral Square</a> during the coronation of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Russia" title="Alexander I of Russia">Alexander I</a>, 1802, by <a href="/wiki/Fyodor_Alekseyev" title="Fyodor Alekseyev">Fyodor Alekseyev</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Moscow_State_University" title="Moscow State University">Moscow State University</a> was established in 1755. Its main building was reconstructed after the 1812 fire by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domenico_Giliardi" title="Domenico Giliardi">Domenico Giliardi</a>. The <i><a href="/wiki/Moskovskiye_Vedomosti" title="Moskovskiye Vedomosti">Moskovskiye Vedomosti</a></i> newspaper appeared from 1756, originally in weekly intervals, and from 1859 as a daily newspaper.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Arbat_Street" title="Arbat Street">Arbat Street</a> had been in existence since at least the 15th century, but it was developed into a prestigious area during the 18th century. It was destroyed in the fire of 1812 and was rebuilt completely in the early 19th century.
</p><p>In the 1830s, general <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Bashilov" title="Alexander Bashilov">Alexander Bashilov</a> planned the first regular grid of city streets north from Petrovsky Palace. <a href="/wiki/Khodynka_Field" title="Khodynka Field">Khodynka field</a> south of the highway was used for military training. Smolensky Rail station (forerunner of present-day <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Belorussky_Rail_Terminal" title="Belorussky Rail Terminal">Belorussky Rail Terminal</a>) was inaugurated in 1870. <a href="/wiki/Sokolniki_Park" title="Sokolniki Park">Sokolniki Park</a>, in the 18th century the home of the tsar's falconers well outside Moscow, became contiguous with the expanding city in the later 19th century and was developed into a public municipal park in 1878. The suburban <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Savyolovsky_Rail_Terminal" title="Savyolovsky Rail Terminal">Savyolovsky Rail Terminal</a> was built in 1902. In January 1905, the institution of the City Governor, or <a href="/wiki/Mayor" title="Mayor">Mayor</a>, was officially introduced in Moscow, and Alexander Adrianov became Moscow's first official mayor.
</p><p>When <a href="/wiki/Catherine_the_Great" title="Catherine the Great">Catherine II</a> came to power in 1762, the city's filth and the smell of sewage were depicted by observers as a symptom of disorderly lifestyles of lower-class Russians recently arrived from the farms. Elites called for improving sanitation, which became part of Catherine's plans for increasing control over social life. National political and military successes from 1812 through 1855 calmed the critics and validated efforts to produce a more enlightened and stable society. There was less talk about the smell and the poor conditions of public health. However, in the wake of Russia's failures in the Crimean War in 1855–56, confidence in the ability of the state to maintain order in the slums eroded, and demands for improved public health put filth back on the agenda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Soviet_period_.281917.E2.80.931991.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Soviet_period_(1917–1991)">Soviet period (1917–1991)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plan_of_Moscow_1917.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="11923" data-file-width="8368" decoding="async" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Plan_of_Moscow_1917.jpg/170px-Plan_of_Moscow_1917.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Plan_of_Moscow_1917.jpg/255px-Plan_of_Moscow_1917.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Plan_of_Moscow_1917.jpg/340px-Plan_of_Moscow_1917.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plan_of_Moscow_1917.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>City plan of Moscow, 1917</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="infobox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.5em 1.5em"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="font-size:115%">External video</th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2" style="text-align: left"><img alt="video icon" data-file-height="128" data-file-width="128" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=bsLRYMM3ORs" rel="nofollow">Song from the Soviet film</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In November 1917, upon learning of <a href="/wiki/October_Revolution" title="October Revolution">the uprising happening in Petrograd</a>, Moscow's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bolshevik" title="Bolshevik">Bolsheviks</a> <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Bolshevik_Uprising" title="Moscow Bolshevik Uprising">also began their uprising</a>. On November 2 (15), 1917, after heavy fighting, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_power" title="Soviet power">Soviet power</a> was established in Moscow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Then <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a>, fearing possible foreign invasion, moved the capital from Petrograd (<a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a>) back to Moscow on March 12, 1918.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prlib_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-prlib-45">[45]</a></sup> The Kremlin once again became the seat of power and the political centre of the new state.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Russian_cultural_heritage_register#Denial_of_heritage_(1917–1941)" title="Russian cultural heritage register">With the change in values imposed by communist ideology</a>, the tradition of preservation of cultural heritage was broken. Independent preservation societies, even those that defended only secular landmarks such as Moscow-based OIRU were disbanded by the end of the 1920s. A new anti-religious campaign, launched in 1929, coincided with the collectivization of peasants; the destruction of churches in the cities peaked around 1932. In 1937 several letters were written to the <a href="/wiki/Central_Committee_of_the_Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Central Committee of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union">Central Committee of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union</a> to rename Moscow to "Stalindar" or "Stalinodar," one from an elderly pensioner whose dream was to "live in Stalinodar" and had selected the name to represent the "gift" (dar) of the genius of Stalin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Stalin rejected this suggestion, and after it was suggested again to him by <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Yezhov" title="Nikolai Yezhov">Nikolai Yezhov</a>, he was outraged, saying "What do I need this for?". This was following Stalin banning the renaming of places in his name in 1936.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USSR" title="USSR">Soviet</a> State Committee of Defence and the General Staff of the <a href="/wiki/Red_Army" title="Red Army">Red Army</a> were located in Moscow. In 1941, 16 divisions of the national volunteers (more than 160,000 people), 25 battalions (18,000 people), and four engineering regiments were formed among the Muscovites. Between October 1941 and January 1942, the German <a href="/wiki/Army_Group_Centre" title="Army Group Centre">Army Group Centre</a> was stopped at the outskirts of the city and then driven off in the course of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Moscow" title="Battle of Moscow">Battle of Moscow</a>. Many factories were evacuated, together with much of the government, and from October 20 the city was declared to be in a state of siege. Its remaining inhabitants built and manned antitank defenses, while the city was bombarded from the air. On May 1, 1944, a medal "For the defence of Moscow" and in 1947 another medal "In memory of the 800th anniversary of Moscow" was instituted.
</p><p>Both <a href="/wiki/German_casualties_in_World_War_II" title="German casualties in World War II">German</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_War_II_casualties_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="World War II casualties of the Soviet Union">Soviet casualties</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Moscow" title="Battle of Moscow">Battle of Moscow</a> have been a subject of debate, as various sources provide somewhat different estimates. Total casualties between September 30, 1941, and January 7, 1942, are estimated to be between 248,000 and 400,000 for the <a href="/wiki/Wehrmacht" title="Wehrmacht">Wehrmacht</a> and between 650,000 and 1,280,000 for the Red Army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stalin%27s_funeral_procession_entering_Manezhnaya_Square_from_Okhotny_Ryad.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="855" data-file-width="1504" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Stalin%27s_funeral_procession_entering_Manezhnaya_Square_from_Okhotny_Ryad.jpg/220px-Stalin%27s_funeral_procession_entering_Manezhnaya_Square_from_Okhotny_Ryad.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Stalin%27s_funeral_procession_entering_Manezhnaya_Square_from_Okhotny_Ryad.jpg/330px-Stalin%27s_funeral_procession_entering_Manezhnaya_Square_from_Okhotny_Ryad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Stalin%27s_funeral_procession_entering_Manezhnaya_Square_from_Okhotny_Ryad.jpg/440px-Stalin%27s_funeral_procession_entering_Manezhnaya_Square_from_Okhotny_Ryad.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stalin%27s_funeral_procession_entering_Manezhnaya_Square_from_Okhotny_Ryad.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Funeral procession at the funeral of Joseph Stalin, 1953</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="infobox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.5em 1.5em"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="font-size:115%">External video</th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2" style="text-align: left"><img alt="video icon" data-file-height="128" data-file-width="128" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=f9p20dxtTkY" rel="nofollow">Stalins USSR in 1953</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>During the postwar years, there was a serious housing crisis, solved by the invention of <a href="/wiki/Tower_block" title="Tower block">high-rise apartments</a>. There are over 11,000 of these standardised and prefabricated apartment blocks, housing the majority of Moscow's population, making it by far the city with the most high-rise buildings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hrranking_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-hrranking-51">[51]</a></sup> Apartments were built and partly furnished in the factory before being raised and stacked into tall columns. The popular Soviet-era comic film <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irony_of_Fate" title="Irony of Fate">Irony of Fate</a></i> parodies this construction method.
</p><p>The city of <a href="/wiki/Zelenograd" title="Zelenograd">Zelenograd</a> was built in 1958 at 37 kilometres (23 miles) from the city centre to the north-west, along with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leningradskoye_Shosse" title="Leningradskoye Shosse">Leningradskoye Shosse</a>, and incorporated as one of Moscow's <a href="/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_Moscow" title="Administrative divisions of Moscow">administrative okrugs</a>. <a href="/wiki/Moscow_State_University" title="Moscow State University">Moscow State University</a> moved to its campus on <a href="/wiki/Sparrow_Hills" title="Sparrow Hills">Sparrow Hills</a> in 1953.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Russian_cultural_heritage_register#Khrushchev's_offensive_(1959–1964)" title="Russian cultural heritage register">In 1959 Nikita Khrushchev launched his anti-religious campaign</a>. By 1964 over 10 thousand churches out of 20 thousand were shut down (mostly in rural areas) and many were demolished. Of 58 monasteries and convents operating in 1959, only sixteen remained by 1964; of Moscow's fifty churches operating in 1959, thirty were closed and six demolished.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parade_outside_of_Hotel_Moscow_1964.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6600" data-file-width="10200" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Parade_outside_of_Hotel_Moscow_1964.jpg/220px-Parade_outside_of_Hotel_Moscow_1964.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Parade_outside_of_Hotel_Moscow_1964.jpg/330px-Parade_outside_of_Hotel_Moscow_1964.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Parade_outside_of_Hotel_Moscow_1964.jpg/440px-Parade_outside_of_Hotel_Moscow_1964.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parade_outside_of_Hotel_Moscow_1964.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Soviet parade outside <a href="/wiki/Four_Seasons_Hotel_Moscow" title="Four Seasons Hotel Moscow">Hotel Moskva</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Manezhnaya_Square,_Moscow" title="Manezhnaya Square, Moscow">Manezhnaya Square</a>, 1964</div></div></div>
<p>On May 8, 1965, due to the actual 20th <a href="/wiki/Victory_Day_(9_May)" title="Victory Day (9 May)">anniversary of the victory</a> in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Moscow was awarded a title of the <a href="/wiki/Hero_City_(Soviet_Union)" title="Hero City (Soviet Union)">Hero City</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_31612_Celebrating_Victory_Day_on_Red_Square.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="660" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/RIAN_archive_31612_Celebrating_Victory_Day_on_Red_Square.jpg/220px-RIAN_archive_31612_Celebrating_Victory_Day_on_Red_Square.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/RIAN_archive_31612_Celebrating_Victory_Day_on_Red_Square.jpg/330px-RIAN_archive_31612_Celebrating_Victory_Day_on_Red_Square.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/RIAN_archive_31612_Celebrating_Victory_Day_on_Red_Square.jpg/440px-RIAN_archive_31612_Celebrating_Victory_Day_on_Red_Square.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_31612_Celebrating_Victory_Day_on_Red_Square.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Victory_Day_(9_May)" title="Victory Day (9 May)">Victory Day</a> celebration on Red Square, May 9, 1975</div></div></div>
<p>The MKAD (ring road) was opened in 1961. It had four lanes running 109 kilometres (68 miles) along the city borders. The MKAD marked the administrative boundaries of the city of Moscow until the 1980s when outlying suburbs beyond the ring road began to be incorporated. In 1980, it hosted the <a href="/wiki/1980_Summer_Olympics" title="1980 Summer Olympics">Summer Olympic Games</a>, which were boycotted by the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and several other Western countries due to the Soviet Union's involvement in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> in late 1979. In 1991 Moscow was the scene of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_coup_attempt_of_1991" title="Soviet coup attempt of 1991">coup attempt</a> by conservative communists opposed to the <a href="/wiki/Perestroika" title="Perestroika">liberal reforms</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev" title="Mikhail Gorbachev">Mikhail Gorbachev</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Recent_history_.281991.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_history_(1991–present)">Recent history (1991–present)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zaryadye_Park1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="760" data-file-width="1142" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Zaryadye_Park1.jpg/220px-Zaryadye_Park1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Zaryadye_Park1.jpg/330px-Zaryadye_Park1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Zaryadye_Park1.jpg/440px-Zaryadye_Park1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zaryadye_Park1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of the Floating bridge in <a href="/wiki/Zaryadye_Park" title="Zaryadye Park">Zaryadye Park</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Red_Square" title="Red Square">Red Square</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moscow_Kremlin" title="Moscow Kremlin">Moscow Kremlin</a> in the distance</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tverskaya_Street2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Tverskaya_Street2.jpg/220px-Tverskaya_Street2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Tverskaya_Street2.jpg/330px-Tverskaya_Street2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Tverskaya_Street2.jpg/440px-Tverskaya_Street2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tverskaya_Street2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tverskaya_Street" title="Tverskaya Street">Tverskaya Street</a>, the main radial street in the city</div></div></div>
<p>When the USSR was dissolved in the same year, Moscow remained the capital of the Russian SFSR (on December 25, 1991, the Russian SFSR was renamed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Federation" title="Russian Federation">Russian Federation</a>). Since then, a market economy has emerged in Moscow, producing an explosion of Western-style retailing, services, architecture, and lifestyles.
</p><p>The city has continued to grow during the 1990s to 2000s, its population rising from below nine to above ten million. Mason and Nigmatullina argue that Soviet-era urban-growth controls (before 1991) produced controlled and sustainable metropolitan development, typified by the greenbelt built in 1935. Since then, however, there has been a dramatic growth of low-density suburban sprawl, created by heavy demand for single-family dwellings as opposed to crowded apartments. In 1995–1997 the MKAD ring road was widened from the initial four to ten lanes.
</p><p>In December 2002 <a href="/wiki/Bulvar_Dmitriya_Donskogo" title="Bulvar Dmitriya Donskogo">Bulvar Dmitriya Donskogo</a> became the first Moscow Metro station that opened beyond the limits of MKAD. The <a href="/wiki/Third_Ring_Road_(Moscow)" title="Third Ring Road (Moscow)">Third Ring Road</a>, intermediate between the early 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Garden_Ring" title="Garden Ring">Garden Ring</a> and the Soviet-era outer ring road, was completed in 2004. The greenbelt is becoming more and more fragmented, and satellite cities are appearing at the fringe. Summer dachas are being converted into year-round residences, and with the proliferation of automobiles there is heavy traffic congestion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Multiple old churches and other examples of architectural heritage that had been demolished during the Stalin era have been restored, such as the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour" title="Cathedral of Christ the Saviour">Cathedral of Christ the Saviour</a>.
In 2010s Moscow's Administration has launched some long duration projects like the <i>Moja Ulitsa</i> (in English: <i>My Street</i>) urban redevelopment program<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> or the Residency renovation one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>By its territorial expansion on July 1, 2012, southwest into the <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Oblast" title="Moscow Oblast">Moscow Oblast</a> the area of the capital more than doubled, going from 1,091 to 2,511 square kilometers (421 to 970 sq mi), resulting in Moscow becoming the largest city on the European continent by area; it also gained an additional population of 233,000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merger2_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Merger2-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mos.ru_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mos.ru-56">[56]</a></sup> The annexed territory was officially named <i>Новая Москва</i> (New Moscow).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Location">Location</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moscow_by_Sentinel-2,_2020-05-11.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7521" data-file-width="7296" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Moscow_by_Sentinel-2%2C_2020-05-11.jpg/220px-Moscow_by_Sentinel-2%2C_2020-05-11.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Moscow_by_Sentinel-2%2C_2020-05-11.jpg/330px-Moscow_by_Sentinel-2%2C_2020-05-11.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Moscow_by_Sentinel-2%2C_2020-05-11.jpg/440px-Moscow_by_Sentinel-2%2C_2020-05-11.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moscow_by_Sentinel-2,_2020-05-11.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Satellite view of Moscow and its nearby suburbs</div></div></div>
<p>Moscow is situated on the banks of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moskva_River" title="Moskva River">Moskva River</a>, which flows for just over 500 km (311 mi) through the <a href="/wiki/East_European_Plain" title="East European Plain">East European Plain</a> in central Russia. <a href="/wiki/List_of_bridges_in_Moscow" title="List of bridges in Moscow">49 bridges</a> span the river and its canals within the city's limits. The <a href="/wiki/Elevation" title="Elevation">elevation</a> of Moscow at the All-Russia Exhibition Center (VVC), where the leading Moscow weather station is situated, is 156 metres (512 feet). <a href="/wiki/Teplostan_Upland" title="Teplostan Upland">Teplostan Upland</a> is the city's highest point at 255 metres (837 feet).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The width of Moscow city (not limiting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MKAD" title="MKAD">MKAD</a>) from west to east is 39.7 km (24.7 mi), and the length from north to south is 51.8 km (32.2 mi).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Time">Time</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Time" title="Moscow Time">Moscow Time</a></div>
<p>Moscow serves as the reference point for the time zone used in most of <a href="/wiki/European_Russia" title="European Russia">European Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Crimea" title="Republic of Crimea">Republic of Crimea</a>. The areas operate in what is referred to in international standards as <i>Moscow Standard Time (MSK, <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">МСК</span></span>)</i>, which is 3 hours ahead of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC" title="UTC">UTC</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC%2B3" title="UTC+3">UTC+3</a>. <a href="/wiki/Daylight_saving_time" title="Daylight saving time">Daylight saving time</a> is no longer observed. According to the geographical <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a> the average <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_noon" title="Solar noon">solar noon</a> in Moscow occurs at 12:30.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Moscow" title="Climate of Moscow">Climate of Moscow</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moscow,_VDNKh,_Central_Alley_after_rain_(30580970263).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2178" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Moscow%2C_VDNKh%2C_Central_Alley_after_rain_%2830580970263%29.jpg/220px-Moscow%2C_VDNKh%2C_Central_Alley_after_rain_%2830580970263%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Moscow%2C_VDNKh%2C_Central_Alley_after_rain_%2830580970263%29.jpg/330px-Moscow%2C_VDNKh%2C_Central_Alley_after_rain_%2830580970263%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Moscow%2C_VDNKh%2C_Central_Alley_after_rain_%2830580970263%29.jpg/440px-Moscow%2C_VDNKh%2C_Central_Alley_after_rain_%2830580970263%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moscow,_VDNKh,_Central_Alley_after_rain_(30580970263).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Exhibition_of_Achievements_of_National_Economy" title="Exhibition of Achievements of National Economy">VDNKh</a> after rain</div></div></div>
<p>Moscow has a <a href="/wiki/Humid_continental_climate" title="Humid continental climate">humid continental climate</a> (<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen</a>: <i>Dfb</i>) with long, cold (although average by Russian standards) winters usually lasting from mid-November to the end of March, and warm summers. More extreme continental climates at the same latitude- such as parts of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Canada" title="Eastern Canada">Eastern Canada</a> or <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>- have much colder winters than Moscow, suggesting that there is still significant moderation from the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> despite the fact that Moscow is far from the sea. Weather can fluctuate widely, with temperatures ranging from −25 °C (−13 °F) in the city and −30 °C (−22 °F) in the suburbs to above 5 °C (41 °F) in the winter, and from 10 to 35 °C (50 to 95 °F) in the summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9F%D0%B5%D1%82%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%BF%D1%83%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D0%B4%D0%B2%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%86_(%D0%B7%D0%B8%D0%BC%D0%B0,_2018).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3506" data-file-width="2713" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/%D0%9F%D0%B5%D1%82%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%BF%D1%83%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D0%B4%D0%B2%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%86_%28%D0%B7%D0%B8%D0%BC%D0%B0%2C_2018%29.jpg/170px-%D0%9F%D0%B5%D1%82%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%BF%D1%83%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D0%B4%D0%B2%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%86_%28%D0%B7%D0%B8%D0%BC%D0%B0%2C_2018%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/%D0%9F%D0%B5%D1%82%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%BF%D1%83%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D0%B4%D0%B2%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%86_%28%D0%B7%D0%B8%D0%BC%D0%B0%2C_2018%29.jpg/255px-%D0%9F%D0%B5%D1%82%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%BF%D1%83%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D0%B4%D0%B2%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%86_%28%D0%B7%D0%B8%D0%BC%D0%B0%2C_2018%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/%D0%9F%D0%B5%D1%82%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%BF%D1%83%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D0%B4%D0%B2%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%86_%28%D0%B7%D0%B8%D0%BC%D0%B0%2C_2018%29.jpg/340px-%D0%9F%D0%B5%D1%82%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%BF%D1%83%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D0%B4%D0%B2%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%86_%28%D0%B7%D0%B8%D0%BC%D0%B0%2C_2018%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9F%D0%B5%D1%82%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%BF%D1%83%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D0%B4%D0%B2%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%86_(%D0%B7%D0%B8%D0%BC%D0%B0,_2018).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Petrovsky_Palace" title="Petrovsky Palace">Petrovsky Palace</a> on <a href="/wiki/Leningradsky_Avenue" title="Leningradsky Avenue">Leningradsky Avenue</a> in winter</div></div></div>
<p>Typical high temperatures in the warm months of June, July, and August are around a comfortable 20 to 26 °C (68 to 79 °F), but during heat waves (which can occur between May and September), daytime high temperatures often exceed 30 °C (86 °F), sometimes for a week or two at a time. In the winter, average temperatures normally drop to approximately −10 °C (14 °F), though almost every winter there are periods of warmth with day temperatures rising above 0 °C (32 °F), and periods of cooling with night temperatures falling below −20 °C (−4 °F). These periods usually last about a week or two. The growing season in Moscow normally lasts for 156 days usually around May 1 to October 5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>The highest temperature ever recorded was 38.2 °C (100.8 °F)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> at the <a href="/wiki/VVC_weather_station" title="VVC weather station">VVC weather station</a> and 39.0 °C (102.2 °F) in the center of Moscow and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domodedovo_airport" title="Domodedovo airport">Domodedovo airport</a> on July 29, 2010, during the unusual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2010_Northern_Hemisphere_summer_heat_waves" title="2010 Northern Hemisphere summer heat waves">2010 Northern Hemisphere summer heat waves</a>. Record high temperatures were recorded for January, March, April, May, July, August, November, and December in 2007–2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pogoda_(data)_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-pogoda_(data)-62">[62]</a></sup> The average July temperature from 1981 to 2010 is 19.2 °C (66.6 °F). The lowest ever recorded temperature was −42.1 °C (−43.8 °F) in January 1940. Snow, which is present for about five months a year, often begins to fall mid-October, while snow cover lies in November and melts at the beginning of April.
</p><p>On average, Moscow has 1731 hours of sunshine per year, varying from a low of 8% in December to 52% from May to August.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> This large annual variation is due to convective cloud formation. In the winter, moist air from the Atlantic condenses in the cold continental interior, resulting in very overcast conditions. However, this same continental influence results in considerably sunnier summers than oceanic cities of similar latitude such as <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>. Between 2004 and 2010, the average was between 1800 and 2000 hours with a tendency to more sunshine in summer months, up to a record 411 hours in July 2014, 79% of possible sunshine. December 2017 was the darkest month in Moscow since records began, with only six minutes of sunlight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Temperatures in the centre of Moscow are often significantly higher than in the outskirts and nearby suburbs, especially in winter. For example, if the average February temperature in the north-east of Moscow is −6.7 °C (19.9 °F), in the suburbs it is about −9 °C (16 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The temperature difference between the centre of Moscow and nearby areas of <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Oblast" title="Moscow Oblast">Moscow Oblast</a> can sometimes be more than 10 °C (18 °F) on frosty winter nights.
</p>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
</div>
<p>Recent changes in Moscow's regional climate, since it is in the mid-latitudes of the northern hemisphere, are often cited by climate scientists as evidence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, though by definition, climate change is global, not regional. During the summer, extreme heat is often observed in the city (2001, 2002, 2003, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2010_Northern_Hemisphere_summer_heat_wave" title="2010 Northern Hemisphere summer heat wave">2010</a>, 2011, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_waves_of_2021#June" title="Heat waves of 2021">2021</a>). Along with a southern part of <a href="/wiki/Central_Federal_District" title="Central Federal District">Central Russia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> after recent years of hot summer seasons, the climate of the city gets <a href="/wiki/Humid_continental_climate#Koppen_Dfa" title="Humid continental climate">hot-summer classification</a> trends. Winter also became significantly milder: for example, the average January temperature in the early 1900s was −12.0 °C (10.4 °F), while now it is about −7.0 °C (19.4 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> At the end of January–February it is often colder, with frosts reaching −30.0 °C (−22.0 °F) a few nights per year (2006, 2010, 2011, 2012, and 2013).
</p><p>The last decade was the warmest in the history of meteorological observations of Moscow. Temperature changes in the city are depicted in the table below:
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population">Population</span></h3>

<p>According to the results of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Census_(2021)" title="Russian Census (2021)">2021 Census</a>, the population of Moscow was 13,010,112;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2021Census_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-2021Census-5">[5]</a></sup> up from 11,503,501 recorded in the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Census_(2010)" title="Russian Census (2010)">2010 Census</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2014_objectively_assess_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-2014_objectively_assess-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>

It is estimated that the proportion of ethnicities in this group is the same as that of the declared group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vital_statistics">Vital statistics</span></h3>
<p>The official population of Moscow is based on those holding "<a href="/wiki/Resident_registration_in_Russia" title="Resident registration in Russia">permanent residency</a>". According to Russia's Federal Migration Service, Moscow holds 1.8 million official "guests" who have temporary residency on the basis of visas or other documentation, giving a legal population of 13.3 million. The number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illegal_immigration_in_Russia" title="Illegal immigration in Russia">Illegal immigrants</a>, the vast majority originating from <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, is estimated to be an additional 1 million people,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> giving a total population of about 14.3 million.
</p><p>Total fertility rate:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>2010 - 1.25</li>
<li>2014 - 1.34</li>
<li>2015 - 1.41</li>
<li>2016 - 1.46</li>
<li>2017 - 1.38</li>
<li>2018 - 1.41</li>
<li>2019 - 1.50</li>
<li>2020 - 1.47
<ul><li>Births (2016): 145,252 (11.8 per 1000)</li>
<li>Deaths (2016): 123,623 (10.0 per 1000)</li></ul></li></ul>

href="/wiki/File:Moscow_Cathedral_Mosque_01-2016.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3618" data-file-width="3618" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Moscow_Cathedral_Mosque_01-2016.jpg/162px-Moscow_Cathedral_Mosque_01-2016.jpg" of the Immaculate Conception</a>; <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Cathedral_Mosque" title="Moscow Cathedral Mosque">Moscow Cathedral Mosque</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Choral_Synagogue" title="Moscow Choral Synagogue">Moscow Choral Synagogue</a></div></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christians</a> form the majority of the city's population; most of whom adhere <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox Church</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Patriarch_of_Moscow_and_all_Rus%27" title="Patriarch of Moscow and all Rus'">Patriarch of Moscow</a> serves as the head of the church and resides in the <a href="/wiki/Danilov_Monastery" title="Danilov Monastery">Danilov Monastery</a>. Moscow was called the "city of 40 times 40 churches"—prior to 1917. Moscow is Russia's capital of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Christianity</a>, which has been the <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Russia" title="Religion in Russia">country's traditional religion</a>.
</p><p>Other religions practiced in Moscow include <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Russia" title="Buddhism in Russia">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism_in_Russia" title="Hinduism in Russia">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Russia" title="Islam in Russia">Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yazidism" title="Yazidism">Yazidism</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rodnovery" title="Rodnovery">Rodnovery</a>. The Moscow Mufti Council claimed that <a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslims</a> numbered around 1.5 million of 10.5 million of the city's population in 2010;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spie_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spie-94">[94]</a></sup> There are four <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">mosques</a> in the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cityscape">Cityscape</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_Moscow" title="List of tallest buildings in Moscow">List of tallest buildings in Moscow</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saint_Basil%27s_Cathedral_in_Moscow.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2218" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Saint_Basil%27s_Cathedral_in_Moscow.jpg/220px-Saint_Basil%27s_Cathedral_in_Moscow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Saint_Basil%27s_Cathedral_in_Moscow.jpg/330px-Saint_Basil%27s_Cathedral_in_Moscow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Saint_Basil%27s_Cathedral_in_Moscow.jpg/440px-Saint_Basil%27s_Cathedral_in_Moscow.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saint_Basil%27s_Cathedral_in_Moscow.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Saint_Basil%27s_Cathedral" title="Saint Basil's Cathedral">Saint Basil's Cathedral</a> in Moscow, a masterpiece of <a href="/wiki/Russian_architecture" title="Russian architecture">Russian architecture</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Museo_Estatal_de_Historia,_Mosc%C3%BA,_Rusia,_2016-10-03,_DD_49.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4681" data-file-width="6255" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Museo_Estatal_de_Historia%2C_Mosc%C3%BA%2C_Rusia%2C_2016-10-03%2C_DD_49.jpg/220px-Museo_Estatal_de_Historia%2C_Mosc%C3%BA%2C_Rusia%2C_2016-10-03%2C_DD_49.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Museo_Estatal_de_Historia%2C_Mosc%C3%BA%2C_Rusia%2C_2016-10-03%2C_DD_49.jpg/330px-Museo_Estatal_de_Historia%2C_Mosc%C3%BA%2C_Rusia%2C_2016-10-03%2C_DD_49.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Museo_Estatal_de_Historia%2C_Mosc%C3%BA%2C_Rusia%2C_2016-10-03%2C_DD_49.jpg/440px-Museo_Estatal_de_Historia%2C_Mosc%C3%BA%2C_Rusia%2C_2016-10-03%2C_DD_49.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Museo_Estatal_de_Historia,_Mosc%C3%BA,_Rusia,_2016-10-03,_DD_49.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/State_Historical_Museum" title="State Historical Museum">State Historical Museum</a>, an example of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revival_architecture" title="Russian Revival architecture">Neo-Russian style</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moscow_July_2011-7a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4516" data-file-width="4940" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Moscow_July_2011-7a.jpg/220px-Moscow_July_2011-7a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Moscow_July_2011-7a.jpg/330px-Moscow_July_2011-7a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Moscow_July_2011-7a.jpg/440px-Moscow_July_2011-7a.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moscow_July_2011-7a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour" title="Cathedral of Christ the Saviour">Cathedral of Christ the Saviour</a>, an example of <a href="/wiki/Neo-Byzantine_architecture_in_the_Russian_Empire" title="Neo-Byzantine architecture in the Russian Empire">Neo-Byzantine architecture</a></div></div></div>
<p>Moscow's architecture is world-renowned. Moscow is the site of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Basil%27s_Cathedral" title="Saint Basil's Cathedral">Saint Basil's Cathedral</a>, with its elegant <a href="/wiki/Onion_dome" title="Onion dome">onion domes</a>, as well as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Savior" title="Cathedral of Christ the Savior">Cathedral of Christ the Savior</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Sisters_(Moscow)" title="Seven Sisters (Moscow)">Seven Sisters</a>. The first Kremlin was built in the middle of the 12th century.
</p><p>Medieval Moscow's design was of concentric walls and intersecting radial thoroughfares. This layout, as well as Moscow's rivers, helped shape Moscow's design in subsequent centuries.
</p><p>The Kremlin was rebuilt in the 15th century. Its towers and some of its churches were built by Italian architects, lending the city some of the aurae of the renaissance. From the end of the 15th century, the city was embellished by masonry structures such as monasteries, palaces, walls, towers, and churches.
</p><p>The city's appearance had not changed much by the 18th century. Houses were made of pine and spruce logs, with shingled roofs plastered with sod or covered by birch bark. The rebuilding of Moscow in the second half of the 18th century was necessitated by constant fires and the needs of the nobility. Much of the wooden city was replaced by buildings in the classical style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>For much of its architectural history, Moscow was dominated by Orthodox churches. However, the overall appearance of the city changed drastically during Soviet times, especially as a result of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a>'s large-scale effort to "modernize" Moscow. Stalin's plans for the city included a network of broad avenues and roadways, some of them over ten lanes wide, which, while greatly simplifying movement through the city, were constructed at the expense of a great number of historical buildings and districts. Among the many casualties of Stalin's demolitions was the <a href="/wiki/Sukharev_Tower" title="Sukharev Tower">Sukharev Tower</a>, a longtime city landmark, as well as mansions and commercial buildings The city's newfound status as the capital of a deeply <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secular" title="Secular">secular</a> nation, made religiously significant buildings especially vulnerable to demolition. Many of the city's churches, which in most cases were some of Moscow's oldest and most prominent buildings, were destroyed; some notable examples include the <a href="/wiki/Kazan_Cathedral,_Moscow" title="Kazan Cathedral, Moscow">Kazan Cathedral</a> and the Cathedral of Christ the Savior. During the 1990s, both were rebuilt. Many smaller churches, however, were lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:0169_-_Moskau_2015_-_Roter_Platz_(25795529393).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3169" data-file-width="4786" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/0169_-_Moskau_2015_-_Roter_Platz_%2825795529393%29.jpg/220px-0169_-_Moskau_2015_-_Roter_Platz_%2825795529393%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/0169_-_Moskau_2015_-_Roter_Platz_%2825795529393%29.jpg/330px-0169_-_Moskau_2015_-_Roter_Platz_%2825795529393%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/0169_-_Moskau_2015_-_Roter_Platz_%2825795529393%29.jpg/440px-0169_-_Moskau_2015_-_Roter_Platz_%2825795529393%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:0169_-_Moskau_2015_-_Roter_Platz_(25795529393).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/GUM_(department_store)" title="GUM (department store)">GUM</a> department store, facing the <a href="/wiki/Red_Square" title="Red Square">Red Square</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ostankino_Tower,_2015.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3571" data-file-width="2384" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Ostankino_Tower%2C_2015.JPG/170px-Ostankino_Tower%2C_2015.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Ostankino_Tower%2C_2015.JPG/255px-Ostankino_Tower%2C_2015.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Ostankino_Tower%2C_2015.JPG/340px-Ostankino_Tower%2C_2015.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ostankino_Tower,_2015.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ostankino_Tower" title="Ostankino Tower">Ostankino Tower</a>, the tallest freestanding structure in Europe, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_freestanding_structures_in_the_world" title="List of tallest freestanding structures in the world">eighth-tallest in the world</a></div></div></div>
<p>While the later Stalinist period was characterized by the curtailing of creativity and architectural innovation, the earlier post-revolutionary years saw a plethora of radical new buildings created in the city. Especially notable were the constructivist architects associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/VKHUTEMAS" title="VKHUTEMAS">VKHUTEMAS</a>, responsible for such landmarks as Lenin's Mausoleum. Another prominent architect was <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Shukhov" title="Vladimir Shukhov">Vladimir Shukhov</a>, famous for Shukhov Tower, just one of many <a href="/wiki/Hyperboloid_structure" title="Hyperboloid structure">hyperboloid towers</a> designed by Shukhov. It was built between 1919 and 1922 as a transmission tower for a Russian broadcasting company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stako_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-stako-98">[98]</a></sup> Shukhov also left a lasting legacy to the <a href="/wiki/Constructivism_(art)" title="Constructivism (art)">Constructivist</a> architecture of early Soviet Russia. He designed spacious elongated shop galleries, most notably the <a href="/wiki/GUM_(department_store)" title="GUM (department store)">GUM</a> department store on <a href="/wiki/Red_Square" title="Red Square">Red Square</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stako_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-stako-98">[98]</a></sup> bridged with innovative metal-and-glass vaults.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moscow,_Hotel_Ukraina_(30585861673).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2178" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Moscow%2C_Hotel_Ukraina_%2830585861673%29.jpg/220px-Moscow%2C_Hotel_Ukraina_%2830585861673%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Moscow%2C_Hotel_Ukraina_%2830585861673%29.jpg/330px-Moscow%2C_Hotel_Ukraina_%2830585861673%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Moscow%2C_Hotel_Ukraina_%2830585861673%29.jpg/440px-Moscow%2C_Hotel_Ukraina_%2830585861673%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moscow,_Hotel_Ukraina_(30585861673).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Sisters_(Moscow)" title="Seven Sisters (Moscow)">Seven Sisters</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radisson_Royal_Hotel,_Moscow" title="Radisson Royal Hotel, Moscow">Hotel Ukraina</a>, is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_Europe" title="List of tallest buildings in Europe">tallest hotel in Europe</a>, and one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_hotels" title="List of tallest hotels">tallest hotels in the world</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zhivopisny_Bridge1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="529" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Zhivopisny_Bridge1.jpg/220px-Zhivopisny_Bridge1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Zhivopisny_Bridge1.jpg/330px-Zhivopisny_Bridge1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Zhivopisny_Bridge1.jpg/440px-Zhivopisny_Bridge1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zhivopisny_Bridge1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Zhivopisny_Bridge" title="Zhivopisny Bridge">Zhivopisny Bridge</a>, the highest cable-stayed bridge in Europe</div></div></div>
<p>Perhaps the most recognizable contributions of the Stalinist period are the so-called <a href="/wiki/Seven_Sisters_(Moscow)" title="Seven Sisters (Moscow)">Seven Sisters</a>, seven massive skyscrapers scattered throughout the city at about an equal distance from the Kremlin. A defining feature of Moscow's skyline, their imposing form was allegedly inspired by the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Municipal_Building" title="Manhattan Municipal Building">Manhattan Municipal Building</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, and their style—with intricate exteriors and a large central spire—has been described as <a href="/wiki/Stalinist_architecture" title="Stalinist architecture">Stalinist Gothic architecture</a>. All seven towers can be seen from most high points in the city; they are among the tallest constructions in central Moscow apart from the <a href="/wiki/Ostankino_Tower" title="Ostankino Tower">Ostankino Tower</a>, which, when it was completed in 1967, was the highest free-standing land structure in the world and today remains the world's seventy-second tallest, ranking among buildings such as the <a href="/wiki/Burj_Khalifa" title="Burj Khalifa">Burj Khalifa</a> in Dubai, <a href="/wiki/Taipei_101" title="Taipei 101">Taipei 101</a> in Taiwan and the <a href="/wiki/CN_Tower" title="CN Tower">CN Tower</a> in Toronto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Soviet goal of providing housing for every family, and the rapid growth of Moscow's population, led to the construction of large, monotonous housing blocks. Most of these date from the post-Stalin era and the styles are often named after the leader then in power (Brezhnev, Khrushchev, etc.). They are usually badly maintained.
</p><p>Although the city still has some five-story apartment buildings constructed before the mid-1960s, more recent apartment buildings are usually at least nine floors tall, and have <a href="/wiki/Elevator" title="Elevator">elevators</a>. It is estimated that Moscow has over twice as many elevators as New York City and four times as many as <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>. Moslift, one of the city's major elevator operating companies, has about 1500 elevator mechanics on call, to release residents trapped in elevators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Stalinist_architecture" title="Stalinist architecture">Stalinist-era buildings</a>, mostly found in the central part of the city, are massive and usually ornamented with <a href="/wiki/Socialist_realism" title="Socialist realism">Socialist realism</a> motifs that imitate <a href="/wiki/Classical_architecture" title="Classical architecture">classical</a> themes. However, small churches—almost always <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox" title="Eastern Orthodox">Eastern Orthodox</a>– found across the city provide glimpses of its past. The Old <a href="/wiki/Arbat_Street" title="Arbat Street">Arbat Street</a>, a tourist street that was once the heart of a bohemian area, preserves most of its buildings from prior to the 20th century. Many buildings found off the main streets of the inner city (behind the Stalinist <a href="/wiki/Fa%C3%A7ade" title="Façade">façades</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tverskaya_Street" title="Tverskaya Street">Tverskaya Street</a>, for example) are also examples of <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">bourgeois</a> architecture typical of Tsarist times. <a href="/wiki/Ostankino_Palace" title="Ostankino Palace">Ostankino Palace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuskovo" title="Kuskovo">Kuskovo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uzkoye" title="Uzkoye">Uzkoye</a> and other large estates just outside Moscow originally belong to nobles from the Tsarist era, and some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Template:Cloisters_of_Moscow" title="Template:Cloisters of Moscow">convents, and monasteries</a>, both inside and outside the city, are open to Muscovites and tourists.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9C%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B0,_%D0%A0%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%81%D0%B8%D1%8F_(Unsplash_-kgrPSetNW8).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2992" data-file-width="3992" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/%D0%9C%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B0%2C_%D0%A0%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%81%D0%B8%D1%8F_%28Unsplash_-kgrPSetNW8%29.jpg/220px-%D0%9C%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B0%2C_%D0%A0%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%81%D0%B8%D1%8F_%28Unsplash_-kgrPSetNW8%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/%D0%9C%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B0%2C_%D0%A0%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%81%D0%B8%D1%8F_%28Unsplash_-kgrPSetNW8%29.jpg/330px-%D0%9C%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B0%2C_%D0%A0%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%81%D0%B8%D1%8F_%28Unsplash_-kgrPSetNW8%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/%D0%9C%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B0%2C_%D0%A0%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%81%D0%B8%D1%8F_%28Unsplash_-kgrPSetNW8%29.jpg/440px-%D0%9C%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B0%2C_%D0%A0%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%81%D0%B8%D1%8F_%28Unsplash_-kgrPSetNW8%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9C%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B0,_%D0%A0%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%81%D0%B8%D1%8F_(Unsplash_-kgrPSetNW8).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Modern methods of skyscraper construction were implemented in the city for the first time with the ambitious <a href="/wiki/Moscow_International_Business_Center" title="Moscow International Business Center">MIBC</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Attempts are being made to restore many of the city's best-kept examples of pre-Soviet architecture. These restored structures are easily spotted by their bright new colors and spotless façades. There are a few examples of notable, early Soviet <a href="/wiki/Avant-garde" title="Avant-garde">avant-garde</a> work too, such as the house of the architect <a href="/wiki/Konstantin_Melnikov" title="Konstantin Melnikov">Konstantin Melnikov</a> in the Arbat area. Many of these restorations were criticized for alleged disrespect of historical authenticity. <a href="/wiki/Facadism" title="Facadism">Facadism</a> is also widely practiced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Later examples of interesting Soviet architecture are usually marked by their impressive size and the semi-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modernist" title="Modernist">Modernist</a> styles employed, such as with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Novy_Arbat" title="Novy Arbat">Novy Arbat</a> project, familiarly known as "false teeth of Moscow" and notorious for the wide-scale disruption of a historic area in central Moscow involved in the project.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Borovitskaya_square1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Borovitskaya_square1.jpg/220px-Borovitskaya_square1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Borovitskaya_square1.jpg/330px-Borovitskaya_square1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Borovitskaya_square1.jpg/440px-Borovitskaya_square1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Borovitskaya_square1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Borovitskaya square, <a href="/wiki/Monument_to_Vladimir_the_Great" title="Monument to Vladimir the Great">Monument to Vladimir the Great</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pashkov_House" title="Pashkov House">Pashkov House</a></div></div></div>
<p>Plaques on house exteriors will inform passers-by that a well-known personality once lived there. Frequently, the plaques are dedicated to Soviet celebrities not well known outside (or often, like with decorated generals and revolutionaries, now both inside) of Russia. There are also many "museum houses" of famous Russian writers, composers, and artists in the city.
</p><p>Moscow's skyline is quickly modernizing, with several new towers under construction.
In recent years, the city administration has been widely criticized for heavy destruction that has affected many historical buildings. As much as a third of historic Moscow has been destroyed in the past few years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> to make space for luxury apartments and hotels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Other historical buildings, including such landmarks as the 1930 Moskva hotel and the 1913 department store Voyentorg, have been razed and reconstructed anew, with the inevitable loss of historical value. Critics blame the government for not enforcing conservation laws: in the last 12 years, more than 50 buildings with monument status were torn down, several of those dating back to the 17th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Some critics also wonder if the money used for the reconstruction of razed buildings could not be used for the renovation of decaying structures, which include many works by architect Konstantin Melnikov<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Mayakovskaya_(Moscow_Metro)" title="Mayakovskaya (Moscow Metro)">Mayakovskaya</a> metro station.
</p><p>Some organizations, such as Moscow Architecture Preservation Society<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> and Save Europe's Heritage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> are trying to draw the international public attention to these problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="graytable" style="text-align:center">
